Ruling on Reciting Bismillah in Prayer: Tilawah vs Tabarruk
Answering the third objection regarding recitation of Tasmiyah in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings of reciting Tasmiyah with the intention of tilawah and tabarruk in Hanafi prayer, and identify the qiyas (analogy) used to justify the latter.

Core Idea
For the Hanafi school, reciting Bismillah in prayer is only permissible as dhikr, not as Quranic recitation.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi response to an objection regarding Bismillah in prayer. The commentator clarifies that the permissibility depends solely on intent: reciting for tilawah is prohibited, while reciting for tabarruk is allowed, analogous to any other form of dhikr. We will explore the analogical reasoning that supports this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two intentions
Recognize that the act is reciting Tasmiyah in prayer, and the two possible intentions are tilawah (Quranic recitation) and tabarruk (seeking blessing).
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each intention
According to the passage, reciting with tilawah intention is not permissible (ghayr jaez), while reciting with tabarruk intention is permissible (jaez).
The ruling is conditional on intention (niyyah).
3
Recognize the analogy to general dhikr
The passage compares reciting Tasmiyah with tabarruk intention to saying SubhanAllah or Alhamdulillah—permissible dhikr forms. This analogy (qiyas) provides the proof for the ruling.
The asl (original case) is general dhikr; the far' (new case) is Tasmiyah with tabarruk.
4
Understand the 'illa that makes the analogy valid
The effective cause ('illa) is the intention of dhikr or tabarruk, not tilawah. Both the asl and far' share this cause, making the analogy sound.
If the intention were tilawah, the 'illa would be absent, and the analogy would not hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[رام؟] تیسری دلیل کا جواب بقصد تلاوت تسمیہ کی تلاوت بھی جائز نہیں تبر کا جائز ہے جس طرح سبحان اللہ ، الحمد للہ و غیرہ کہنا۔
Answer to the third proof: With the intention of recitation, reciting Tasmiyah is not permissible; with the intention of Tabarruk (seeking blessing) it is permissible, just like saying SubhanAllah, Alhamdulillah, and other such phrases.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection raised against the Hanafi position on reciting Bismillah in prayer. The commentator clarifies that the permissibility depends on intent. If one recites Bismillah with the intention of tilawah (Quranic recitation), it is not permissible in prayer because Bismillah is not considered a separate verse in the Hanafi school for the purpose of recitation. However, if one recites it with the intention of tabarruk (seeking blessing), it is permissible just like any other dhikr such as SubhanAllah or Alhamdulillah. This ruling is based on the principle that the legality of an act in prayer is determined by the intention behind it.

Key Terms
تسمیہ (Tasmiyah): The phrase 'Bismillahir Rahmanir Rahim' (In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful).
تبرک (Tabarruk): Seeking blessing; here, the intention of reciting Tasmiyah for barakah rather than as Quranic recitation.
تلاوت (Tilawah): Recitation of the Quran; here, the intention of reciting Tasmiyah as part of Quranic recitation.
قیاس (Qiyas): Analogical reasoning; the method used to extend the ruling of general dhikr to Tasmiyah with tabarruk intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on reciting Bismillah with the intention of tilawah (recitation) in prayer?
A) Permissible
B) Impermissible
C) Permissible only if one is in a state of wudu
D) Permissible only for the imam

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, reciting Bismillah in prayer is only permissible with the intention of tabarruk (seeking blessing), not with the intention of tilawah (Quranic recitation).

Definition of Quran: Both Words and Meanings
Defining Quran as comprising both nazm and ma'na

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the Quran as comprising both nazm and ma'na and evaluate the two common misconceptions about its nature.

Core Idea
The Quran is both the Arabic words and their meanings — neither alone suffices.

This passage tackles a foundational question in usul al-fiqh: what exactly is the Quran? The author clarifies that it is a combination of the Arabic verbal composition (nazm) and the intended meanings (ma'na). He addresses two misconceptions: that the Quran is only words or only meanings. Understanding this definition is crucial for rulings on recitation in prayer and the nature of divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central definition
The matn states that the Quran is a name for both nazm and ma'na together. Understand this as the core thesis.
Nazm = verbal composition; ma'na = meaning.
2
Recognize the two misconceptions
The commentator explains that two wrong ideas are being refuted: the Quran is only words (based on properties like writing) or only meanings (based on a reported fatwa).
These misconceptions arise from partial evidence.
3
Trace the refutation of the meanings-only view
The commentator firmly denies that Imam Abu Hanifa held that view, citing the Quranic verse 'Arabic Quran' as proof.
The verse is a strong textual dalil.
4
Apply the definition to the fatwa issue
Understand that even if a ruling about prayer in translation existed, it would not change the definition of Quran. The definition remains both words and meanings.
Distinguish between rukhsah and definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وهو اسم للنظم والمعنى جميعاً ماتن رحمہ اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قرآن مجید لفظ اور معانی دونوں کے مجموعے کا نام ہے کسی ایک کا نہیں۔ مقصد عبارت : در سری بات یہی ہے کہ یہ ایک وہم کا دفعیہ ہے۔ وہم کا مبنی ماتن رحمہ اللہ کی عبارت قراردی جاسکتی ہے کیونکہ انہوں نے کتاب اللہ کی تعریف میں انزال کتابت اور نقل کا ذکر کیا ہے اور یہ تمام محض لفظوں کی صفات ہیں چنانچہ کسی کو یہ وہم ہو سکتا ہے کہ شاید دور آن صرف الفاظ کا نام ہے اور معانی کو اس میں کوئی حیثیت حاصل نہیں۔ اس طرح وہم امام ابو حنیفہ رحمہ اللہ کی طرف منسوب ایک فتوی سے بھی پیدا ہوتا ہے جس میں انہوں نے فرمایا ہے کہ کوئی شخص عربی زبان اور قرآة قرآن پر قادر ہونے کے باوجود کسی دوسری زبان میں قرار پانکرے تو نماز ادا ہو جائیگی اس سے یہ وہم ابھر کر سامنے آتا ہے کہ شاید قرآن فقط معانی کا نام ہے الفاظ عربیہ کو اسمیں کوئی مقام حاصل نہیں ۔ چنانچہ ماتن رحمہ اللہ نے واضح کر دیا کہ قرآن لفظ اور معنی دونوں کے مجموعے کا نام ہے نہ فقط الفاظ کا جس طرح تعریف قرآن میں مذکور صفات سے وہم ہو سکتا ہے اور نہ فقط معانی کا ۔ یہ علیحدہ بحث ہے کہ امام صاحب رحمہ اللہ نے فتوی دیا یا نہیں (اگر دیا ہے تو کیوں؟ درست یہی ہے کہ ایسا کوئی فتوی حضرت امام اعظم نے نہیں دیا گھر کوئی انعصر ار کرے تو اس کے جوابات بھی دیئے گئے ہیں جن کا ماحصل یہ ہے کہ امام اعظم رحمہ اللہ کا یہ مذہب قطعاً نہیں کہ قرآن مجید صرف معانی کا نام ہے۔ اگر ائیں بات ہوتی تو وہ نماز کے علاوہ بھی ایسا قول کرتے حالانکہ ان سے ایسی بات ثابت نہیں کی جا سکتی ، کبہ بھی کیسے سکتے ہیں جبکہ اللہ تعالٰی نے واضح طور پر فرمارہے ہیں قرآن عربی میں
His saying: 'And it (Quran) is a name for both the words and meanings together.' The author (al-Matin) says that the Noble Quran is a name for the combination of both words and meanings, not just one. The purpose of the text: This is essentially a refutation of a misconception. The basis of the misconception can be ascribable to the author's own wording because in his definition of the Book of Allah, he mentioned 'sending down', 'writing', and 'transmission', all of which are properties of mere words. Thus one might misunderstand that the Quran is only a name for the words, and meanings have no status in it. Similarly, a misconception arises from a fatwa attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa in which he said that if a person capable of Arabic and Quranic recitation recites the translation in another language, the prayer is still valid. From this, it might be wrongly concluded that the Quran is only meanings, and the Arabic words have no place. So the author clarified that the Quran is the name for both words and meanings together, not only words (as might be misunderstood from the definition's attributes) nor only meanings. This is a separate discussion whether Imam Abu Hanifa actually gave such a fatwa. The correct view is that Imam A'zam never gave such a fatwa. If someone insists, there are refutations. The summary is that it is definitely not the Imam's position that the Quran is only meanings. If it were, he would have said so in contexts other than prayer, but such a statement cannot be proven from him. How could it be, when Allah explicitly says: 'The Quran is in Arabic?'
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definition-focused discussion. The matn says that the Quran is a name for both nazm (verbal form) and ma'na (meaning). The commentator explains that this statement is necessary to counter two possible misconceptions: firstly, that the Quran is only words (based on the definition mentioning transmission etc.), and secondly, that it is only meanings (based on a reported fatwa from Imam Abu Hanifa allowing prayer in translation). The commentator firmly refutes the attribution of such a fatwa to Imam Abu Hanifa, asserting that the Imam's position is that the Quran is the combination of specific Arabic words and their meanings. The final proof is the Quranic verse 'We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran.' Thus the Quran is inherently Arabic in both words and meanings.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): The verbal composition of the Quran, i.e., its words in the specific Arabic order.
معنی (ma'na): The meaning intended by the words of the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position clarified in this passage, what is the Quran?
A) Only the words (nazm)
B) Only the meanings (ma'na)
C) Both words and meanings together
D) The oral recitation only

Key Takeaway
The Quran is both the Arabic words and their meanings; neither alone suffices.

Mahr al-Mithl for a Mufawwidah: Immediate Obligation by Contract
Ruling on mahr al-mithl for mufawwidah with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that mahr al-mithl is wajib immediately upon the marriage contract for a mufawwidah, contrast it with the Shafi'i position, and analyze the legal reasoning behind the obligation.

Core Idea
For a mufawwidah, the marriage contract itself makes mahr al-mithl wajib, even before consummation or death.

This lesson explores a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the timing of mahr obligation when no specific mahr is agreed. The Hanafi school holds that the marriage contract itself necessitates a proper dower (mahr al-mithl), while the Shafi'i school ties it to consummation or a guardian's involvement. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi view on the binding nature of the marriage contract and the role of custom (urf) in determining default obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The matn states 'mahr al-mithl becomes wajib by the contract itself in the mufawwidah.' Identify that the subject is a mufawwidah (woman married without specified mahr).
2
Understand the definition of mufawwidah
The commentary defines mufawwidah: either a guardian gave her in marriage without mahr, or she herself (if adult) contracted without mahr. This clarifies who falls under this ruling.
The definition excludes cases where mahr is specified or where there is no valid contract.
3
Contrast the Shafi'i position
According to the text, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that without a guardian, mahr is not wajib upon the contract; it becomes wajib only upon consummation or death of a spouse. Identify the key difference in the condition for obligation.
Note that the Shafi'i view also considers the presence of a guardian (wali) as necessary for the contract to have full effect.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle
The Hanafi position is based on the principle that marriage is a contract of exchange (mu'awada), and mahr is a necessary consequence. Therefore, mahr al-mithl is due immediately upon a valid contract. Understand how the school derives this from the nature of the contract.
This analogy to sale contracts is a key usul move: if the price is not stated, the market price is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وجب مهر المثل بنفس العقد في المفوضة

چھٹی تفریح

یہ ہے کہ عورت جو مفوضہ ہو یعنی جس کو اس کے ولی نے بغیر مہر کے کسی کے حوالے کر دیا ہو یا یہ خود ہو کہ لا مهر لها تو امام شافعی کے نزدیک ولی کے بغیر اس کا مہر واجب نہیں ہوگا۔ لہذا اگر وطی سے پہلے دونوں میں سے ایک کا انتقال ہو گیا تو مفوضہ کے لئے مہر واجب ہوتا ۔
His statement: Mahr al-mithl becomes obligatory by the contract itself in the case of mufawwidah.

Sixth branch.

This is that a woman who is mufawwidah, i.e., one whom her guardian has handed over to someone without mahr, or she herself is such that she has no mahr, then according to Imam al-Shafi'i, without a guardian, her mahr is not obligatory. So if before intercourse one of the two dies, then for the mufawwidah, mahr becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on mahr (dower) for a mufawwidah—a woman married without specification of mahr. The matn states that mahr al-mithl (the proper dower of her peers) is wajib immediately by the marriage contract itself. The commentary expands this by clarifying the definition of mufawwidah: either a guardian married her off without fixing a mahr, or she herself (if adult) made the contract without mahr. The ruling contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that without a guardian, mahr is not wajib upon the contract; instead, it becomes wajib only upon consummation or death of one spouse. The Hanafi position is that once the marriage contract is valid, mahr al-mithl is due immediately, even if the marital relationship is not yet consummated. This is based on the principle that marriage is a contract of exchange, and mahr is a necessary consequence of the contract when not specified.

Key Terms
مفوضہ (mufawwidah): A woman married without the mahr being specified in the contract; the mahr is delegated to be determined later or is absent.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The dower equivalent to that of women similar to the bride in age, beauty, family status, etc., used when no specific mahr is agreed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does mahr al-mithl become wajib for a mufawwidah?
A) Only after consummation of marriage.
B) Only after the death of one spouse.
C) Immediately upon the marriage contract itself.
D) Only if the woman demands it afterwards.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, mahr al-mithl is wajib immediately upon the marriage contract for a mufawwidah, based on the principle that marriage is a contract of exchange requiring a proper dower.

Definition of 'Amr' (Command) in Hanafi Usul
Defining 'amr' with its components and addressing a linguistic objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'amr' (command) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, identify its three components, and distinguish it from linguistic and other usul categories.

Core Idea
An amr in Shariah is the superior's 'Do!' that obligates, not a peer's request.

This lesson unpacks the precise definition of 'amr' (command) as a foundational technical term in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Unlike everyday language, where 'amr' can include requests and supplications, the Shariah definition narrows it to a command from a superior to an inferior, using the imperative form. Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving rulings from commands in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus (jins) in the definition
The definition begins with 'qawl' (statement), which is the broad category. All commands are statements, but not all statements are commands.
'Qawl' includes any verbal utterance.
2
Identify the differentia (fasl) — condition of superiority
The phrase 'ala sabili al-isti'la' (in a manner of superiority) narrows 'qawl' to only those statements made by a superior to an inferior, excluding requests and supplications.
This is the key difference from linguistic definitions.
3
Identify the specific form (sigha)
The word 'if'al' (Do!) specifies that the command must be in the imperative form, which excludes prohibitions (which use 'la taf'al').
The imperative must be a derived form from the present tense.
4
Analyze the objection from grammarians
The passage raises an objection: grammarians include requests and supplications under 'amr', so the superiority condition would be unnecessary if the linguistic definition were used. The Hanafi usul definition is a technical one for legal reasoning, distinct from pure grammar.
This objection highlights the need for technical definitions in usul.
5
Apply the definition
Given a Quranic or hadith imperative, determine if it qualifies as an amr by checking: is it a statement? Is the speaker superior? Is it in imperative form? If yes, it creates an obligation (unless there is evidence to the contrary).
Practice with examples like 'أقيموا الصلاة' (Establish prayer).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومنه الامر و هو قول القائل لغيره على سبيلا لاستعلاء افعل
ماقیل سے ربط
خاص کی وہ اقسام جو شریعت میں بکثرت استعمال کی جاتی ہیں ان میں سے ایک قسم امر ہے اب ماتن رحمہ اللہ امر کی بحث کا آغاز فرمارہے ہیں۔
امر کی تعریف
فوائد قیمود
قول القائل لغيره على سبيل الاستعلاء افعل
قائل کا اپنے غیر کو استعلاء کے طریقے پر افعل کہتا ۔
اس تعریف میں ( قول ) جنس ہے تمام الفاظ کو شامل ہے (علی سبیل الاستعلاء ) کی قید سے التماس اور دعا کو خارج کیا اور (افعل ) کی قیدت نہی خارج ہو گیا کیونکہ اسمیں لا تفعل ہوتا ہے۔
(فعل) سے مراد وہ لفظ ہے جو معروف طریقے کے مطابق مضارع سے مشتق ہوشر ط صرف یہ ہیکہ اس سے مقصود فعل کو لازم کرتا ہو۔ اور دوسرا یہ کہ کہنے والا اپنے آپ کو برا سمجھے۔ اعتراض .... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اگر اس امر سے اہل عربیت کی اصطلاح مراد ہے تو پر ملی ہیں ان ستعال کی قید گانا درست نہیں کیونکہ ان کے نزدیک اتنہاس اور دعا ،امر میں داخل ہے لہذا ان کو نکالنے کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے ۔ اور اگر اس سے اصول فقہ وانوں ائل مانگی تهدید سے مرا دی راه
His statement: 'And among them is 'amr', and it is the statement of a speaker to another in a manner of superiority: 'Do'.' Connection to what was said: Among the specific categories that are frequently used in Shariah, one is 'amr'. Now the author (may Allah have mercy on him) begins the discussion of 'amr'. Definition of 'amr': 'The statement of a speaker to another in a manner of superiority: 'Do'.' In this definition, 'statement' (qawl) is the genus, including all words. The condition 'in a manner of superiority' excludes request and supplication. And the condition 'Do' excludes prohibition, as it contains the negation. By 'Do' (fi'l) is meant a word derived from the present tense in the known manner, with the condition that it intends to make the action necessary, and the speaker considers himself superior. Objection: If by 'amr' the terminology of Arabic grammarians is intended, then the condition of superiority is incorrect because in their view request and supplication are included in 'amr', so excluding them is useless. And if by it the terminology of usul al-fiqh is intended, then [the text cuts off].
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'amr' (command) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The definition consists of three components: statement (qawl), manner of superiority (isti'la'), and the specific imperative verb 'if'al'. The definition excludes request (iltimas) and supplication (du'a) via the superiority condition, and excludes prohibition (nahy) via the 'if'al' condition. The text then raises an objection: if the linguistic definition (of the grammarians) is intended, the superiority condition is problematic because grammarians include request and supplication under 'amr'. The implication is that the usul definition is distinct, focusing on the authoritative command from a superior to an inferior. The passage remains incomplete, but the core definition is established.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The statement of a speaker to another in a manner of superiority: 'Do'.
استعلاء (isti'la'): A manner of superiority, where the speaker considers himself of higher status than the addressee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the condition 'on the way of superiority' (على سبيل الاستعلاء) exclude from the definition of 'amr'?
A) Prohibition (نهي)
B) Request (التماس) and supplication (دعاء)
C) Imperative (فعل)
D) Statement (قول)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'amr' is 'the statement of a superior to an inferior using the imperative form,' which obligates the commanded act.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
6 min
Command Indicates Obligation, Not Recommendation
Stating the rule that command indicates obligation and beginning the dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a command (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) by default, identify the proofs mentioned in the text, and contrast the first opposing view that it indicates recommendation (nadb).

Core Idea
A command demands compliance; the default is obligation, not recommendation.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the interpretation of commands in the Quran and Sunnah. Understanding whether a command imposes an obligation or merely a recommendation shapes the entire derivation of rulings. The text establishes the default rule and begins the discussion of scholarly disagreement, starting with the view that a command indicates recommendation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
Read the opening statement: 'موجبه الوجوب لا الندب والاباحة والتوقف' – the requirement of a command is obligation, not recommendation, permission, or suspension. This is the Hanafi default.
The rule applies whether the command comes after a prohibition or before.
2
List the Proofs Mentioned
The text gives three proofs: (1) textual evidence (nass) – the one commanded is not left indifferent; (2) blame is deserved for abandonment; (3) consensus (ijma) and reason ('aql). Note each proof and how it supports wujub.
The phrase 'لانتفاء الغيرة عن المامور' indicates that the one commanded cannot be indifferent.
3
Note the Exclusions
The author explicitly negates other possibilities: nadb (recommendation), ibaha (permission), and tawaqquf (suspension). Understand that the default is obligation, not any lower category.
These exclusions are foundational for subsequent debates.
4
Recognize the Disagreement
The text states that many fuqaha disagree. The first opposing opinion is that amr indicates nadb (recommendation). Identify that this is the beginning of the ikhtilaf discussion.
The text cuts off, but the student should understand that the Hanafi position is wujub, while others hold nadb.
5
Apply the Principle
Consider how this principle is used: when encountering an imperative in the Quran or Sunnah, the Hanafi usuli presumes wujub unless a qarina (indicator) shifts it to nadb or another category.
This is a key difference from some other schools that may treat the default as permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وموجبه الوجوب لا الندب والاباجة والتوقف سواء
كار بعد الحظر أو قبله لانتفاء الغيرة عن المامور بالامر بالنص واستحقاق الوحيد لتاركه ولدلالة الاجماع والمعقول پھر مصنف رحمہ اللہ نے اشتراک کی نفی کی اور فرمایا کہ امر کا موجب صرف وجوب ہے اس میں بہت سے فقہاء کا اختلاف ہے
(1) پہلا نہ ہب بعض لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ اس کا موجب ندب ہے۔
His statement: 'And its requirement is obligation, not recommendation, nor permission, nor suspension — whether (the command comes) after a prohibition or before it — because of the absence of indifference from the one commanded regarding the command, by textual evidence, and the entitlement of blame for the one who abandons it, and due to the indication of consensus and reason.' Then the author (may Allah have mercy on him) negated commonality and said that the command's requirement is only obligation. There is much disagreement among jurists regarding this. (1) The first opinion: Some people say that its requirement is recommendation.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage establishes a fundamental usul principle: the command (amr) necessarily indicates obligation (wujub), not recommendation (nadb), permission (ibaha), or suspension (tawaqquf). The author mentions several proofs: textual evidence (nass) that the one commanded is not left indifferent (i.e., the command demands compliance), that the one who abandons it is worthy of blame, and that consensus (ijma) and reason ('aql) support this. Then the author notes that many fuqaha disagree; the first opinion mentioned is that the command indicates recommendation (nadb). The text here is the beginning of a discussion of the ikhtilaf, but it is cut off. This principle is foundational in Hanafi usul, as it determines how commands in the Quran and Sunnah are interpreted.

Key Terms
امر (amr): A command (typically imperative form) that requires compliance.
ندب (nadb): Recommendation; an act whose performance is rewarded but not obligatory.
اباحة (ibaha): Permission; an act that is neither rewarded nor punished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the default requirement (muqtada) of a command (amr)?
A) Recommendation (nadb).
B) Permission (ibaha).
C) Obligation (wujub).
D) Suspension (tawaqquf).

Key Takeaway
The command (amr) by default indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul, supported by textual evidence, blame for abandonment, consensus, and reason.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Qur'anic Proofs that Amr Indicates Wujub
Presentation of two Qur'anic proofs for amr = wujub with refutation of objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Qur'anic proofs (Surah al-Ahzab 33:36 and Surah al-Nur 24:63) and explain how each establishes that the imperative (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
These verses show that a command from Allah and His Messenger removes choice and threatens punishment – obligation is the only consistent reading.

In this lesson, we delve into the first two Qur'anic proofs that the Hanafi school uses to establish that the imperative (amr) primarily indicates obligation (wujub). The author counters objections and presents clear textual evidence: the verse about believers having no choice when Allah and His Messenger decree, and the verse threatening those who oppose the Prophet's command. We will analyze the logical structure of these proofs and understand why the Hanafi position is considered strong.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first proof and its textual basis
Locate the verse from Surah al-Ahzab (33:36) in the source text. Understand the phrase 'Wama kana limu'minin wa la mu'minatin...' and note that it implies a command from Allah and His Messenger leaves no option for the believer.
2
Analyze the implication of the first proof
The verse states that when Allah and His Messenger have decreed a matter, the believer has no choice. This directly implies that the command is obligatory; otherwise, there would still be a choice. Understand the logical connection.
Hanafi usul holds that the absence of choice (takhyir) indicates wujub.
3
Identify the second proof and its textual basis
Locate the verse from Surah al-Nur (24:63) in the source text: 'Falyahdhari alladhina yukhalifuna an amrihi...' This verse warns of punishment for those who oppose the Prophet's command.
4
Analyze the implication of the second proof
The threat of punishment for opposition only makes sense if the command is obligatory. If it were merely recommended or permissible, opposition would not warrant punishment. This reinforces the principle.
The threat (wa'id) is a strong indicator of wujub.
5
Address the objection regarding permissibility from hunting
The author responds to the counterexample of the verse 'Wa idha halaltum fastadu' (when you are free, hunt). He argues that permissibility is derived from other verses (uhilla lakum al-tayyibat), not from the imperative itself. Also, if hunting were obligatory, it would cause hardship, contradicting its purpose.
The imperative after prohibition is by consensus for obligation.

In refuting the proof of those people [opponents], we say that in the Noble Qur'an, even after prohibition, the imperative has come for obligation, as He said: 'Then when the sacred months have passed, slay the idolaters wherever you find them...etc.' The method of inference is that first Allah Almighty forbade fighting in the four months of Rajab, Dhul-Hijjah, Dhul-Qa'dah, and Muharram, and after that prohibition He issued an imperative which, by consensus, is for obligation. As for permissibility (from 'fas'tadu' (hunt)) it does not come, but rather it is understood from 'uhilla lakum al-tayyibat' (allowed to you are good things). The second point is that if hunting were obligatory, hardship would necessarily arise, whereas permission for hunting is given for the benefit of the Hindus. Next, the author is giving proofs on the madhhab of the Hanafis. First proof: The imperative comes for obligation. For this, the first proof is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And it is not for a believing man or a believing woman...etc.' The meaning of this verse is that when Allah and His Messenger (peace be upon him) command something, the believer's choice of acceptance or rejection ceases and acting upon it becomes obligatory. And this is only when the imperative is for obligation. Second proof: In the Holy Qur'an: 'And let those beware who oppose his command, lest a trial befall them or a painful punishment.' That is, those who oppose the command of the Honorable Prophet (peace be upon him) should fear trial in this world and punishment in the Hereafter. In this verse, those who oppose the command of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) are deemed deserving of threat, and this threat is only valid when we say they have left an obligation. Objection: What is the proof that in this verse the imperative is for obligation? Answer: We reply that the content and context of the verse indicate that the imperative is for obligation. Second objection: Then an objection is made that in this verse opposition could also mean denial? Answer: We say that among the Arabs, 'opposition' (mukhalafah) is applied to the abandonment of action because it is the opposite of compliance, i.e., action. A logical objection to the mentioned proof: From the mentioned proof (the verse 'falyahdhari...'), circular reasoning (musadara alal matlub) results, meaning the principle is that the claim depends on the proof, and accepting the proof necessitates the claim. But in the mentioned verse, the establishment of the proof depends on accepting the claim: in that the proof contains the imperative (falyahdhari), and the proof will only be valid when we accept this imperative to be for obligation, whereas there is itself a debate about whether it is for obligation or not. When the proof itself is not established, how can the claim be established? The explanation of circular reasoning is that the proof will be valid only when we accept the claim (that the imperative is for obligation), whereas the actual dispute is about this claim. Answer: Your saying that 'falyahdhari...' is debated regarding the imperative being for obligation is not correct; there is no debate or obscurity about it. Rather, it is only for obligation, as proven by the context of the speech. Therefore, the proof does not depend on the claim such that circular reasoning would result. Rather, the proof is established, and the establishment of the proof necessitates the establishment of the claim. Thus our claim is also established, and it is that the imperative is for obligation. Third proof: The author (may Allah have mercy on him) has taken as the third proof for the imperative being for obligation the indication of Ijma, the summary of which is that there is consensus on this matter: that whenever someone is asked for something...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative expansion from a Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh commentary. The author refutes the position that the imperative (amr) can indicate something other than obligation (wajib), specifically responding to objections. He first addresses an opponent's proof that after prohibition, an imperative still indicates obligation, using the verse about killing idolaters after the sacred months. He argues that the imperative after prohibition is for obligation by consensus. Then he clarifies that permissibility (ibaha) is derived from other verses. The author then presents three proofs for the Hanafi position: First, the verse 'Wa ma kana li mu'minin wa la mu'minatin...' which states that when Allah and His Messenger decree a matter, believers have no choice, implying the imperative is obligatory. Second, the verse 'Falyahdhari alladhina yukhalifuna an amrihi...' which threatens those who oppose the Prophet's command, and this threat only makes sense if the command is obligatory. The author then addresses two objections: one about the proof from context, and another about whether 'mukhalafah' means denial or abandonment. He concludes that 'mukhalafah' means abandonment of action, not denial. Then a logical objection is raised: the second proof involves circular reasoning (musadara alal matlub) because it assumes the imperative is for obligation to prove that it is for obligation. The author rebuts this by stating that the context of the verse clearly indicates obligation, so the proof stands on its own. Finally, the third proof is introduced: Ijma (consensus) that the imperative indicates obligation, though the details are cut off at the page break. The Hanafi school holds that the default (asl) in the imperative is obligation unless there is a qarinah (contextual indicator) to the contrary.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): A linguistic form that requests an action from a higher to a lower authority. In usul, it is the focus of whether it indicates obligation or another meaning.
وجوب (wujub): The legal classification that an action is obligatory, meaning it must be performed and its omission is punishable.
خيرة (khayrah): Choice or option. In the verse, negation of khayrah indicates that the command is binding.
وعيد (wa'id): Threat of punishment. The presence of wa'id for opposition indicates that the command is obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi proof, what does the verse 'Wama kana limu'minin wa la mu'minatin...' establish about the imperative?
A) That the imperative indicates permissibility (ibaha) because it follows a prohibition.
B) That the imperative indicates obligation (wujub) because believers have no choice when Allah and His Messenger command.
C) That the imperative indicates recommendation (nadb) because it is from Allah.
D) That the imperative indicates a general request without legal force.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school proves that the imperative (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) using two Qur'anic verses: one negates choice after a divine decree, and the other threatens punishment for opposition.

Refuting the Circular Reasoning Objection: The Role of Context
Refutation of the circular reasoning objection against the second proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circular reasoning (musadara alal matlub) objection against the second proof (Surah al-Nur 24:63) and explain the Hanafi response that context (siyaq) independently establishes obligation.

Core Idea
The context of the verse itself, not the claim, proves that the command is for obligation – no circularity.

In this lesson, we focus on a sophisticated logical objection raised against the second proof (the verse threatening opposition). Opponents claim that using this verse as proof for amr = wujub involves circular reasoning: the proof only works if we already accept that the imperative is for obligation. The Hanafi response is decisive: the context (siyaq) of the verse clearly indicates obligation, independent of the claim. Understanding this rebuttal strengthens your grasp of how usul scholars defend their principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the circular reasoning objection
The opponent argues: To prove that amr = wujub using 'Falyahdhari...', you must assume that the imperative in that verse is for obligation. But that is exactly what is disputed. So the proof presupposes the claim – circular reasoning.
This is a standard logical critique in usul al-fiqh debates.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The author replies that the imperative in the verse is not disputed – the context (siyaq) makes it clear that it is for obligation. There is no need to assume the claim; the context itself establishes obligation.
The context includes the threat of punishment, which only makes sense if the command is obligatory.
3
Analyze how context (siyaq) functions as independent evidence
The siyaq (context) includes the threatened punishment. This threat is a qarinah that indicates the command is binding. Therefore, the proof does not depend on the general claim about amr, but on the specific context of this verse.
In Hanafi usul, siyaq can serve as a qarinah to determine the meaning of a word.
4
Appreciate the methodological implication
This shows that in usul, a proof must be based on independent evidence, not on the very point under dispute. The Hanafi response demonstrates that the verse is self-sufficient in indicating obligation due to its context.
This strengthens the Hanafi position because it avoids logical fallacy.

Source Text

دیتے ہیں کہ آیت کا مضمون اور سیاق بتاتا ہے کہ امر وجوب کے لئے ہے۔ دوسرا اعتراض ... پھر اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اس آیت میں مخالفت سے انکاری مخالفت بھی تو مراد ہوسکتی ہے؟ جواب ہم کہتے ہیں کہ عربوں کے ہاں مخالفت کا اطلاق ترک عمل پر ہوتا ہے کیونکہ یہ موافقت یعنی عمل کی ضد ہے۔ دلیل مذکور پر ایک قومی اعتراض: مذكورة دليل ( فليحذر الذين يخالفون عن امره ... الخ ) سے تو مصادره علی المطلوب لازم آتا ہے یعنی اصول یہ ہے کہ دعوئی دلیل پر موقوف ہوا کرتا ہے اور دلیل تسلیم کرنے سے دعوی کا ما ننالازم ہو جاتا ہے مگر مذکورہ آیت میں دلیل کا اثبات دعوی کے ماننے پر موقوف ہے وہ اس طور پر کہ دلیل میں امر مذکور ہے (خلیجز ر ) اور دلیل تب درست ہوگی جب ہم اس امر کو وجوب کیلئے مان لیں حالانکہ اسکے وجوب یا عدم وجوب میں خود کلام ہے جب دلیل ہی ثابت نہیں تو دعوئی کیونکر ثابت ہوگا مصادر و علی المطلوب کی وضاحت یہ ہے کہ دلیل تب درست ہو گی جب ہم دعوئی (الامر للوجوب) تسلیم کر لیں حالانکہ اصل نزاع اس دعوی میں ہے۔ جواب آپ کا یہ کہنا درست نہیں کہ فليحذر ..... الخ امر کے وجوب میں کلام ہے اس میں کوئی کلام اور خفا نہیں بلکہ اس کا فقط وجوب کیلئے ہوتا سیاق کلام سے ثابت ہے لہذا دلیل دعوئی پر موقوف نہیں کہ مصادرہ علی المطلوب لازم آتا ہو بلکہ دلیل ثابت ہے اور اثبات دلیل مستلزم ہے اثبات دعوی کو پس ہما را دعوی بھی ثابت ہو گیا اور وہ ہے الامر للوجوب ۔ تیسری دلیل : مصنف رحمہ اللہ نے امر کے وجوب کیلئے ہونے پر تیسری دلیل دلالت اجماع کو قرار دیا ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اس امر پر اجماع ہے کہ جب بھی کسی سے کوئی مٹی طلب کی
In refuting the proof of those people [opponents], we say that in the Noble Qur'an, even after prohibition, the imperative has come for obligation, as He said: 'Then when the sacred months have passed, slay the idolaters wherever you find them...etc.' The method of inference is that first Allah Almighty forbade fighting in the four months of Rajab, Dhul-Hijjah, Dhul-Qa'dah, and Muharram, and after that prohibition He issued an imperative which, by consensus, is for obligation. As for permissibility (from 'fas'tadu' (hunt)) it does not come, but rather it is understood from 'uhilla lakum al-tayyibat' (allowed to you are good things). The second point is that if hunting were obligatory, hardship would necessarily arise, whereas permission for hunting is given for the benefit of the Hindus. Next, the author is giving proofs on the madhhab of the Hanafis. First proof: The imperative comes for obligation. For this, the first proof is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And it is not for a believing man or a believing woman...etc.' The meaning of this verse is that when Allah and His Messenger (peace be upon him) command something, the believer's choice of acceptance or rejection ceases and acting upon it becomes obligatory. And this is only when the imperative is for obligation. Second proof: In the Holy Qur'an: 'And let those beware who oppose his command, lest a trial befall them or a painful punishment.' That is, those who oppose the command of the Honorable Prophet (peace be upon him) should fear trial in this world and punishment in the Hereafter. In this verse, those who oppose the command of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) are deemed deserving of threat, and this threat is only valid when we say they have left an obligation. Objection: What is the proof that in this verse the imperative is for obligation? Answer: We reply that the content and context of the verse indicate that the imperative is for obligation. Second objection: Then an objection is made that in this verse opposition could also mean denial? Answer: We say that among the Arabs, 'opposition' (mukhalafah) is applied to the abandonment of action because it is the opposite of compliance, i.e., action. A logical objection to the mentioned proof: From the mentioned proof (the verse 'falyahdhari...'), circular reasoning (musadara alal matlub) results, meaning the principle is that the claim depends on the proof, and accepting the proof necessitates the claim. But in the mentioned verse, the establishment of the proof depends on accepting the claim: in that the proof contains the imperative (falyahdhari), and the proof will only be valid when we accept this imperative to be for obligation, whereas there is itself a debate about whether it is for obligation or not. When the proof itself is not established, how can the claim be established? The explanation of circular reasoning is that the proof will be valid only when we accept the claim (that the imperative is for obligation), whereas the actual dispute is about this claim. Answer: Your saying that 'falyahdhari...' is debated regarding the imperative being for obligation is not correct; there is no debate or obscurity about it. Rather, it is only for obligation, as proven by the context of the speech. Therefore, the proof does not depend on the claim such that circular reasoning would result. Rather, the proof is established, and the establishment of the proof necessitates the establishment of the claim. Thus our claim is also established, and it is that the imperative is for obligation. Third proof: The author (may Allah have mercy on him) has taken as the third proof for the imperative being for obligation the indication of Ijma, the summary of which is that there is consensus on this matter: that whenever someone is asked for something...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative expansion from a Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh commentary. The author refutes the position that the imperative (amr) can indicate something other than obligation (wajib), specifically responding to objections. He first addresses an opponent's proof that after prohibition, an imperative still indicates obligation, using the verse about killing idolaters after the sacred months. He argues that the imperative after prohibition is for obligation by consensus. Then he clarifies that permissibility (ibaha) is derived from other verses. The author then presents three proofs for the Hanafi position: First, the verse 'Wa ma kana li mu'minin wa la mu'minatin...' which states that when Allah and His Messenger decree a matter, believers have no choice, implying the imperative is obligatory. Second, the verse 'Falyahdhari alladhina yukhalifuna an amrihi...' which threatens those who oppose the Prophet's command, and this threat only makes sense if the command is obligatory. The author then addresses two objections: one about the proof from context, and another about whether 'mukhalafah' means denial or abandonment. He concludes that 'mukhalafah' means abandonment of action, not denial. Then a logical objection is raised: the second proof involves circular reasoning (musadara alal matlub) because it assumes the imperative is for obligation to prove that it is for obligation. The author rebuts this by stating that the context of the verse clearly indicates obligation, so the proof stands on its own. Finally, the third proof is introduced: Ijma (consensus) that the imperative indicates obligation, though the details are cut off at the page break. The Hanafi school holds that the default (asl) in the imperative is obligation unless there is a qarinah (contextual indicator) to the contrary.

Key Terms
مصادره علی المطلوب (musadara alal matlub): Circular reasoning: when the proof for a claim depends on the claim itself. The author refutes that this applies to the second proof.
سياق (siyaq): Context of a passage that helps determine its intended meaning. In this lesson, siyaq (including threat of punishment) indicates obligation.
وعيد (wa'id): Threat of punishment. Used as evidence for the obligation of the command that is violated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key evidence the Hanafi school uses to refute the circular reasoning objection?
A) The verse is from the Qur'an.
B) The threat of punishment shows the imperative is for obligation, independent of the claim.
C) The imperative appears in a command form.
D) The objection is not valid because it comes from an opponent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school successfully refutes the circular reasoning objection by demonstrating that the context (siyaq) of the verse independently establishes that the imperative is for obligation.

Third and Fourth Proofs for al-Amr li al-Wujub and the Haqiqah/Majaz Debate
Continuing proofs that command indicates obligation, then discussing haqiqah for ibahah/nadb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the third and fourth proofs for al-amr li al-wujub, explain the distinction between ijma and dalalat al-ijma, and evaluate the two opinions on using amr for ibahah/nadb.

Core Idea
The imperative indicates obligation by consensus of usage and rational necessity; departing from that meaning is metaphorical unless context demands otherwise.

We have already seen two proofs that the imperative (amr) indicates obligation (wujub). This passage presents two more: the indication of consensus (dalalat al-ijma) and a rational proof. It also addresses a nuanced objection about circular reasoning and then opens a debate: when the imperative is used for permission or recommendation, is that literal or metaphorical? Understanding these proofs is essential to applying the Hanafi default rule that amr is for wujub.

Guided Path
1
Follow the objection and response on circular reasoning
An opponent claims that the verse 'Falyahzar...' leads to circular reasoning: the proof depends on accepting that the command is for obligation, which is exactly the point in dispute. The author responds that the context of the verse itself shows the command is for obligation, so the proof stands independent.
This objection is known as 'musadarah ala al-matlub' (begging the question).
2
State the third proof: dalalat al-ijma
The author argues that there is consensus (ijma) on the method of making a demand: whenever something is requested, it is requested using the imperative form. Since the essence of a demand is obligation, this implies that the imperative indicates obligation.
The author warns against saying there is ijma that the imperative itself indicates obligation; rather, the consensus is on the method, and from that the obligation is inferred.
3
Explain the fourth rational proof
General actions have specific meanings. The imperative is a general action used for requesting, so it should have a specific meaning—obligation. Additionally, a master commands his slave; if the slave disobeys, he deserves punishment only if the command was obligatory. This shows the imperative indicates obligation.
The master-slave analogy is a common rational argument in Hanafi usul.
4
Identify the two opinions on amr for ibahah/nadb
The passage presents two views: (1) using amr for permission or recommendation remains literal (haqiqah) because those are part of the meaning of command (they are also types of request); (2) it is metaphorical (majaz) because it departs from the original meaning of obligation.
The text does not give a tarjih (preference) here; it simply records the ikhtilaf.

تیسری دلیل : مصنف رحمہ اللہ نے امر کے وجوب کیلئے ہونے پر تیسری دلیل دلالت اجماع کو قرار دیا ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ اس امر پر اجماع ہے کہ جب بھی کسی سے کوئی مٹی طلب کی جائے تو صیغہ امر کے ذریعہ طلب کی جائے کیونکہ مطالبہ کا کوئی اور طریقہ ہے ہی نہیں جس طرح ہم روز مرہ استعمال اور عرف میں مشاہدہ کرتے رہتے ہیں۔ اور طلب میں اصل کمال طلب ہے جو وجوب ہی کے ذریعہ تحقیق ہو سکتا ہے۔
یہ دائم نہ کیا جائے کہ امر کے وجوب کیلئے ہونے پر اجماع ہے، اکثر طلبہ اس مقام پر دلالت اجماع کی بجائے ( اجماع ) کہہ دیا کرتے ہیں جو ایک بڑی غلطی ہے کیونکہ اگر اس امر پر اجماع ہوتا کہ امر وجوب کے لئے ہے تو پھر اختلاف کیونکر ہوتا حالانکہ آپ چار مختلف مذاہب مع الدلائل ملاحظہ کر چکے ہیں یا درکھئے کہ اجماع تو اس بات پر ہے کہ طلب صیغہ امر سے ہوگی اور اس کے واسطے سے دلاتا امر کا وجوب کیلئے ہونا معلوم ہے
چوتھی دلیل عقلی عقل اس بات پر دلالت کرتی ہے کہ امر صرف وجوب کے لئے ہو، اس لئے کہ عام افعال کسی مخصوص معنے پر دلالت کرتے ہیں تو مناسب یہ ہے کہ صیغہ امر بھی وجوب یعنی ایک مخصوص معنی کے لئے ہو ۔
اور بعض لوگوں نے کہا کہ عقلی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب آقا اپنے غلام کو کسی کام کا حکم دے اور وہ نہ کرے تو وہ سزا کا مستحق ہوتا ہے اور وہ سزا کا مستحق اس وقت ہوگا جب امر وجوب کے لئے ہو۔ کیونکہ کسی امر مندوب یا مستحب کا ترک موجب عتاب و عقاب نہیں ہو سکتا۔ قولة واذا اريدت به الاباحة أو الندب فقيل انه حقيقة لانه بعضه وقيل لا لانه جاوز اصله .
تشریح عبارت:
اور اگر امر سے اس کا معنی حقیقی یعنی وجوب ترک کر کے اباحت یا ندب کا معنی مراد لیا جائے تو اس میں دو قول ہیں:
پہلا قول یہ ہے کہ بعض لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ یہ حقیقت سے ہو گا اس لئے کہ اباحت اور ندب بھی بعض معنی ہیں
Objection: What is the proof that in this verse the command is for obligation? Answer: We say the context of the verse indicates the command is for obligation. Second objection: Could the opposition in the verse refer to denial of opposition? Answer: We say among the Arabs, opposition is applied to leaving the action because it is the opposite of compliance. An objection on the mentioned proof: From the verse 'Let those who oppose his command beware...' circular reasoning follows, meaning that the claim depends on the proof, and the proof is valid when we accept the claim. But in the verse, the proof's establishment depends on accepting the claim because the command is mentioned and the proof is valid only if we accept the command is for obligation, while its obligation is disputed. The response: It is incorrect that there is dispute about the command's obligation; the context shows it is only for obligation. So the proof does not depend on the claim, but the proof is established and establishes the claim, thus our claim that the command is for obligation is proven. Third proof: The author gives the third proof as indication of consensus, which is that there is consensus that whenever something is requested, it is requested by the imperative form, because there is no other method of request, as we observe in daily usage and custom. And in request, the essence of request is obligation. One should not say there is consensus that the command is for obligation; many students say 'consensus' instead of 'indication of consensus', which is a big mistake, because if there were consensus that the command is for obligation, how could there be disagreement? Rather, consensus is that request is by the imperative form, and through that the command's indication of obligation is known. Fourth rational proof: Reason indicates that the command is only for obligation, because general actions indicate a specific meaning, so it is appropriate that the imperative form also indicates obligation, a specific meaning. Some said the rational proof is that when a master commands his slave to do something and he does not do it, he deserves punishment, and he would deserve punishment only if the command is for obligation, because leaving a recommended act does not incur punishment. Statement: And if the command is intended for permission or recommendation, some say it is still literal because it is a part of it, and others say it is not because it has departed from its original meaning. Explanation of the phrase: And if by the command its literal meaning of obligation is abandoned and the meaning of permission or recommendation is intended, then there are two opinions: The first is that some say it is literal because permission and recommendation are also some meanings...
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the discussion proving that the imperative (amr) indicates obligation (wujub). It begins with an objection and response regarding the verse 'Falyahzar alladhina yukhalifuna 'an amrihi' (Quran 24:63), addressing a charge of circular reasoning. The author argues that the context of the verse clearly shows the command is for obligation, thus the proof is independent. The third proof is from the indication of consensus (dalalat al-ijma): there is consensus that when something is demanded, it is demanded using the imperative form, and the essence of demand is obligation. Importantly, the author warns against saying there is consensus that the command itself is for obligation, as that would negate the existence of differing schools. Rather, the consensus is on the use of the imperative for demand, which then indicates obligation. The fourth proof is rational: general actions have specific meanings, so the imperative should be for a specific meaning (obligation). Another rational proof is the master-slave example: a slave deserves punishment for not obeying a command, which only makes sense if the command is obligatory. The passage then transitions to the issue of using the imperative for permission (ibahah) or recommendation (nadb). Two opinions are presented: one holds it remains literal (haqiqah) because these meanings are part of the command's scope, and the other says it becomes metaphorical (majaz) because it departs from its original meaning of obligation. The author's discussion reflects Hanafi usul methodology, emphasizing contextual and rational arguments.

Key Terms
امر (amr): The imperative form used for requesting an action
وجوب (wujub): Obligation; a ruling that demands performance with punishment for omission
اباحت (ibahah): Permission; a ruling that allows an action without reward or punishment
ندب (nadb): Recommendation; a ruling that encourages an action without obligation
دلالت اجماع (dalalat al-ijma): Indication of consensus; the use of consensus as a proof that the imperative indicates obligation, based on the agreed method of demand
مصادرہ علی المطلوب (musadarah ala al-matlub): Circular reasoning; a fallacy where the proof depends on the claim it is supposed to prove

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it incorrect to say there is consensus that the imperative form indicates obligation?
A) Because consensus cannot be established on linguistic matters.
B) Because if such consensus existed, there would be no disagreement among scholars on this issue.
C) Because the imperative is only used for obligation in the Quran.
D) Because consensus always requires rational proof.

Key Takeaway
The imperative indicates obligation by default, supported by consensus on the method of demand and rational reasoning; departure to permission or recommendation requires a contextual indicator.

The Command Form and Obligation: Proof and Debate on Permissibility/Recommendation
Explaining the Hanafi proof that amr is for obligation and the debate on its usage for ibaha or nadb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi proof that amr indicates wujub, distinguish between ijma and dalalat al-ijma, and explain the two views on whether amr used for ibaha or nadb is haqiqah or majaz.

Core Idea
The command form indicates obligation by default; its use for permissibility is either literal (shared genus) or metaphorical (different differentia).

This passage addresses a foundational debate in Hanafi usul: does the command form (sigha al-amr) inherently signify obligation (wujub)? The author presents a rational proof based on the principle that words signal specific meanings, and a practical argument from the master-slave paradigm. Crucially, he corrects a common student confusion: the consensus (ijma) is on the method of demand through amr, not on its specific indication of wujub. The passage then examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: when amr is used for ibaha or nadb, is that usage literal (haqiqah) because they share the genus of permissibility of action, or metaphorical (majaz) because they differ in the differentia (prohibition vs. permissibility of leaving)? Both positions are presented neutrally.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational proof for amr's indication of wujub
Identify the argument that words generally indicate specific meanings, so the command form should indicate the specific meaning of obligation.
2
Recognize the role of consensus
Understand that the consensus is on the method of demand through amr, not on its specific indication of wujub. Many students mistakenly claim ijma for the latter; avoid this error.
3
Grasp the master-slave analogy
The punishment for disobedience only makes sense if the command is obligatory, reinforcing the default wujub indication.
4
Analyze the haqiqah/majaz debate
When amr is used for ibaha or nadb, one view considers it haqiqah because ibaha/nadb share the genus of permissibility of action with obligation; the other view considers it majaz because the differentia contradicts.
5
Appreciate the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf
Both views are legitimate within the Hanafi school; the text does not prefer one, reflecting the depth of Hanafi usul.

جائے تو صیغہ امر کے ذریعہ طلب کی جائے کیونکہ مطالبہ کا کوئی اور طریقہ ہے ۔ ہی نہیں جس طرح ہم روز مرہ استعمال اور عرف میں مشاہدہ کرتے رہتے ہیں۔ اور طلب میں اصل کمال طلب ہے جود جوب ہی کے ذریعہ تحقیق ہو سکتا ہے۔
کھتے یہ دائم نہ کیا جائے کہ امر کے وجوب کیلئے ہونے پر اجماع ہے، اکثر طلبہ اس مقام پر دلالت اجماع کی بجائے ( اجماع ) کہہ دیا کرتے ہیں جو ایک بڑی غلطی ہے کیونکہ اگر اس امر پر اجماع ہوتا کہ امرد جوب کے لئے ہے تو پھر اختلاف کیونکر ہوتا حالانکہ آپ چار مختلف مذاہب مع الدلائل ملاحظہ کر چکے ہیں یا درکھئے کہ اجماع تو اس بات پر ہے کہ طلب صیغہ امر سے ہوگی اور اس کے واسطے سے دلاتا امر کا وجوب کیلئے ہونا معلوم ہے
چوتھی دلیل عقلی عقل اس بات پر دلالت کت تی ہے کہ امرصف وجوب کے لئے ہو، اس لئے کہ عام افعال کسی مخصوص معنے پر دلالت کرتے ہیں تو مناسب یہ ہے کہ صیغہ امر بھی وجوب یعنی ایک مخصوص معنی کے لئے ہو ۔
اور بعض لوگوں نے کہا کہ مقلی سلیل یہ ہے کہ جب آقا اپنے غلام کو کسی کام کا حکم دے در دو نہ کرے تو وہ سزا کا مستحق ہوتا ہے اور وہ سزا کا مستحق اسوقت ہوگا جب امر وجوب کے لئے ہو۔ کیونکہ کسی امر مندوب یا مستحب کا ترکب موجب عتاب و عقاب نہیں ہو سکتا۔ قولة واذا اريدت به الاباحة أو الندب فقيل انه حقيقة لأ نه بعضه وقيل لا لأنه جاوز اصله .
تشریح عبارت:
اور اگر امرت اس کا معنی حقیقی یعنی وجوب ترک کر کے اباحت یا ندب کا معنیٰ مراد لیا جا ئے تو اس میں دو نہ ہب ہیں:
پہلا نے جب بعض لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ یہ حقیقت سے ہو گا اس لئے کہا باحت اور ندب وجوب کا بعض ہیں اور کسی چیز کا بعض اس کی حقیقت قاصرد ہوتا ہے کیونکہ جو کرنے میں جو از فعل مع حرمت ترک کو اور اباحت کہتے ہیں جواز فعل مع جواز ترک کو اور ندب کہتے ہیں جواز فعل مع رجحان انفعل کو مذہب علامہ فخر الاسلام علی بن محمد نسفی رحمہ اللہ کا ہے۔
دوسران آب بعض کہتے ہیں کہ جب امرا باحت یا ندب کے لئے استعمال ہوگا تو یہ مجاز ہوگا کیونکہ یہ اپنی حقیقت اور اصل سے تجاوز کر گیا ہے اس لئے کہ وجوب کہتے ہیں جو از فعل مع حرمت ترک کو جبکہ اباحت ، ندب دونوں میں جواز فعل کے ساتھ ساتھ جو از ترک ہوتا ہے اور جو از ترک حرمت ترک سے متضاد و متجاوز ہے۔
خلاصه کلام
خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ جن لوگوں نے وجوب کی تعریف میں صرف جنس یعنی جواز لعل کو دیکھا انہوں نے کہہ دیا یہ حقیقت ہے اور جن لوگوں نے جنس اور فصل دونوں کو دیکھا انہوں نے کہا کہ مجاز
امر مقتضى الحمرار یا محتمل لقرار ہے یا نہیں
Then the command form is used for requesting because there is no other method of demand, just as we observe in daily usage and custom. And in request, the essence of demand is obligation, which can only be realized through obligation. They say: Do not mistakenly say that there is consensus that the command is for obligation. Many students say 'consensus' instead of 'indication of consensus', which is a big mistake, because if there were consensus that the command is for obligation, then how would there be disagreement, while you have seen four different schools with proofs. Understand that consensus is that demand occurs through the command form, and through that it is known that the command indicates obligation. The fourth proof: reason indicates that the command is for obligation, because general actions indicate specific meanings, so it is appropriate that the command form is also for obligation, i.e., a specific meaning. And some have said that the rational proof is that when a master commands his slave to do something and he does not do it, he deserves punishment. And he deserves punishment only when the command is for obligation, because the omission of a recommended or desirable act does not cause blame and punishment. Statement: 'And if it is used for permissibility or recommendation, it is said it is literal because it is part of it, and it is said it is not because it has departed from its original meaning.' Explanation of the text: And if the command is used with the meaning of permissibility or recommendation, abandoning its literal meaning of obligation, then there are two views: First, some say it will be literal because permissibility and recommendation are parts of obligation, and part of a thing is its incomplete reality. The definition of obligation is permissibility of action with prohibition of leaving, permissibility is permissibility of action with permissibility of leaving, and recommendation is permissibility of action with preference for action. This is the position of Allamah Fakhr al-Islam Ali ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi. Second, others say that when the command is used for permissibility or recommendation, it will be metaphorical because it has departed from its literal and original meaning. Because obligation is defined as permissibility of action with prohibition of leaving, while both permissibility and recommendation include permissibility of leaving along with permissibility of action, and permissibility of leaving contradicts and opposes prohibition of leaving. Summary: The summary is that those who looked only at the genus (permissibility of action) in the definition of obligation said it is literal, while those who looked at both genus and differentia said it is metaphorical.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of the command form (amr) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author clarifies that the command form is primarily used to indicate obligation (wujub), and this is known through rational proof and consensus on the method of demand, not on the specific ruling of obligation. The fourth rational proof argues that words typically convey specific meanings, so amr should signify the specific meaning of obligation. Additionally, the master-slave analogy shows that punishment for non-compliance only makes sense if the command is obligatory. The passage then addresses a secondary issue: when amr is used for permissibility (ibaha) or recommendation (nadb), is that usage literal or metaphorical? Two views are presented: (1) It is literal because ibaha and nadb are parts or incomplete forms of obligation (genus only). (2) It is metaphorical because they differ from obligation in the differentia (prohibition of leaving vs. permissibility of leaving). The author does not give preference but presents both views accurately.

Key Terms
الامر (al-amr): The command form (sigha) that primarily indicates obligation (wujub) by way of demand.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Obligation: permissibility of action with prohibition of leaving (jawaz al-fi'l ma'a hurmat al-tark).
الاباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility: permissibility of action with permissibility of leaving (jawaz al-fi'l ma'a jawaz al-tark).
الندب (al-nadb): Recommendation: permissibility of action with preference for performing it (jawaz al-fi'l ma'a rujhan al-fi'l).
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Literal usage: using a word for its original meaning as placed by language.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage: using a word for a meaning other than its original due to a relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rational proof presented, why must the command form (amr) indicate obligation?
A) Because of consensus of the companions
B) Because general actions indicate specific meanings, and amr is a type of action
C) Because the master punishes the slave for not obeying a command, which only makes sense if the command is obligatory
D) Because of the Quranic verse 'Obey Allah and the Messenger'

Key Takeaway
The command form indicates obligation by default, supported by rational proof and practical necessity, and its use for permissibility or recommendation is subject to interpretation as literal or metaphorical within the Hanafi school.

Defining Wujub, Ibahah, and Nadb: The Haqiqah/Majaz Debate
Defines wujub, ibahah, nadb and discusses whether amr for ibahah/nadb is haqiqah or majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wujub, ibahah, and nadb, and apply the linguistic analysis of jins and fasl to determine whether amr used for ibahah/nadb is haqiqah or majaz according to the Hanafi usul dispute.

Core Idea
Whether amr for ibahah/nadb is literal depends on whether you define wujub by its genus alone or by its full differentia.

This passage from Nur al-Anwar presents three foundational legal categories—wujub, ibahah, nadb—and then explores an internal Hanafi dispute: when the imperative (amr) is used for ibahah or nadb, is it used literally (haqiqah) or figuratively (majaz)? The answer hinges on how we define wujub. Imam al-Nasafi takes one view, while other scholars hold a different opinion based on including or excluding the specific differentia (fasl) of each category. This lesson will parse the definitions and the reasoning behind both positions.

Guided Path
1
Define the three terms
Parse the definitions of wujub, ibahah, and nadb as given. Note the shared genus (jins: permissibility of action) and the distinct differentiae (fasl: prohibition/permissibility/preference).
Use the literal definitions from the text.
2
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the dispute is about whether amr used for ibahah/nadb is haqiqah or majaz. The text presents two views.
3
Analyze al-Nasafi's position
Understand the argument that if only jins is considered, amr is haqiqah for all three categories because they share the genus 'permissibility of action'.
4
Analyze the opposing position
Understand the argument that if both jins and fasl are considered, amr is haqiqah only for wujub (whose fasl includes prohibition of omission) and majaz for ibahah/nadb (whose fasl includes permissibility of omission, contradicting prohibition).
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf: how the interpretation of amr in texts of ibahah/nadb affects legal inference regarding obligation vs. recommendation/permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ندب وجوب کا بعض ہے اور کسی چیز کا بعض اس کی حقیقت قاصر ہوتا ہے کیونکہ وجوب کہتے ہیں جواز فعل مع حرمت ترک کو اور اباحت کہتے ہیں جواز فعل مع جواز ترک کو اور ندب کہتے ہیں جواز فعل مع رجحان فعل کو۔ یہ مذہب علامہ فخر الاسلام علی بن محمد نسفی رحمہ اللہ کا ہے۔ دوسرے بعض کہتے ہیں کہ جب امر اباحت یا ندب کے لئے استعمال ہوگا تو یہ مجاز ہوگا کیونکہ یہ اپنی حقیقت اور اصل سے تجاوز کر گیا ہے اس لئے کہ وجوب کہتے ہیں جواز فعل مع حرمت ترک کو جبکہ اباحت، ندب دونوں میں جواز فعل کے ساتھ ساتھ جواز ترک ہوتا ہے اور جواز ترک حرمت ترک سے متضاد ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ جن لوگوں نے وجوب کی تعریف میں صرف جنس یعنی جواز فعل کو دیکھا انہوں نے کہہ دیا یہ حقیقت ہے اور جن لوگوں نے جنس اور فصل دونوں کو دیکھا انہوں نے کہا کہ مجاز۔
Nadb is a part of wujub, and a part of something is deficient in its reality. Because wujub is called the permissibility of action with prohibition of omission, and ibahah is called the permissibility of action with permissibility of omission, and nadb is called the permissibility of action with preference of the action. This is the madhhab of 'Allamah Fakhr al-Islam 'Ali bin Muhammad al-Nasafi. Others among them say that when amr is used for ibahah or nadb, it is majaz because it has transcended its original reality, because wujub is defined as permissibility of action with prohibition of omission, while in ibahah and nadb, along with permissibility of action, there is also permissibility of omission, and permissibility of omission contradicts prohibition of omission. The summary: those who looked only at the genus (jins) in the definition of wujub (i.e., permissibility of action) said it is haqiqah, and those who looked at both genus and differentia (fasl) said it is majaz.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage opens with a definition of three key legal categories (wujub, ibahah, nadb) as understood by Imam al-Nasafi. It then presents an internal Hanafi dispute about whether the imperative (amr) when used for ibahah or nadb is haqiqah (literal) or majaz (figurative). The dispute hinges on how one defines wujub: if only the common element (jins) of permissibility of action is considered, then amr is haqiqah for all three; if the specific differentia (fasl) of each is included, then amr is haqiqah only for wujub and majaz for the others. This is a linguistic usul analysis.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujub): Permissibility of action with prohibition of omission.
اباحت (ibahah): Permissibility of action with permissibility of omission.
ندب (nadb): Permissibility of action with preference for doing it.
حقیقت (haqiqah): A word used in its original linguistic sense.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a sense other than its original due to a relation.
جنس (jins): Genus; the common element in a definition.
فصل (fasl): Differentia; the specific element distinguishing a category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Al-Nasafi says it's majaz, opponents say haqiqah.
B) Al-Nasafi says it's haqiqah because he only considers jins; opponents say majaz because they consider both jins and fasl.
C) Both say haqiqah but for different reasons.
D) Both say majaz but for different reasons.

Key Takeaway
The literalness of amr for ibahah/nadb depends on whether one defines wujub by its genus alone or by its full definition including the differentia.

Ada' (Performance): Definition and the Command vs. Time Objection
Definition and defense of ada' as obligation from command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ada' and explain why the obligation is not negated by the time condition.

Core Idea
Ada' is delivering the exact wajib commanded; the command establishes obligation, time sets performance window.

This lesson unpacks the definition of ada' from the matn, clarifying the relationship between divine command and time. A common objection is that obligation is time-bound, but the passage demonstrates that the command is the source of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the phrase 'taslīm `ayn al-wājib bi al-amr' and note its components.
The term 'bi al-amr' attaches to 'taslīm', not to 'wājib'.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that obligation is tied to time, so how can it be 'with the command'? This is a common misunderstanding.
3
Analyze the response
The commentator resolves by parsing the Arabic: 'bi al-amr' qualifies 'taslīm', not 'wājib'. Thus delivery is with the command; obligation's attachment is not addressed.
This preserves the authority of the command while acknowledging time as a condition for performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وحكم الأمر نوعان أداء وهو تسليم عين الواجب بالأمر قضاء وهو تسليم مثل الواجب أمر سے وجوب ثابت ہوتا ہے اس کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) وجوب أداء یہ ہے کہ امر سے جو کچھ واجب ہو اس کو بعینہ بلا تغیر و تبدل ادا کرنا۔ علامہ فخر الاسلام وغیرہ نے أداء کی تعریف یہ کی ہے (تسلیم نفس الواجب بالأمر) اس پر اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ نفس وجوب امر کے ساتھ نہیں ہوتا بلکہ وہ تو وقت کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے۔ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ (بالأمر) تسلیم کے ساتھ متعلق ہے واجب کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں ہے تو مطلب یہ ہوا کہ تسلیم کرنا یہ امر کے ساتھ متعلق ہے باقی یہ کہ وجوب کس کے ساتھ ہے امر کے ساتھ یا وقت کے ساتھ اس کے بارے میں یہاں کوئی بات نہیں ہوئی بلکہ سکوت ہے۔ (۲) وجوب قضاء یہ ہے کہ مثل واجب کو امر کے ساتھ ادا کرنا " تسليم مثل الواجب بالأمر " نکتہ : بعض لوگوں نے کہا کہ یہاں پر (من عنده) کی قید لگانا چاہیے کیونکہ اگر کوئی شخص آج ظہر کی نماز کل کی ظہر کی نماز کی قضاء ادا کرے تو یہ جائز نہیں ہے۔
His statement: 'And the ruling of the command is of two types: ada' which is delivering the very object of the obligation with the command, and qada' which is delivering the like of the obligation.' From the command, obligation is established. Its two types: (1) Obligation of ada': It is to perform the very thing that becomes obligatory from the command without change or alteration. Imam Fakhr al-Islam and others defined ada' as 'delivering the very object of the obligation with the command.' There is an objection: the very obligation is not with the command but with the time. Answer: The answer is that 'with the command' is attached to 'delivering,' not to the 'obligation.' So the meaning is that delivering is attached to the command. As for whether the obligation is with the command or with time, there is no discussion here; it is silent. (2) Obligation of qada': It is to deliver the like of the obligation with the command: 'delivering the like of the obligation with the command.' Point: Some have said that one should add the condition 'from himself' (min 'indihi) here, because if someone today performs qada' of today's Zuhr prayer for yesterday's Zuhr prayer, it is not valid.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a definition and expansion of two types of obligation arising from a command (amr) in usul al-fiqh. The matn states that the ruling of a command is twofold: ada' (performance) and qada' (make-up). Ada' is performing the exact act commanded, while qada' is performing similar act (like in kind) at a later time. The commentator, likely from a sharh of Nur al-Anwar, explains that obligation is proven by the command. He then clarifies the definition of ada' given by Fakhr al-Islam, which uses 'delivering the very wajib with the command'. An objection arises: since obligation is tied to time (e.g., Zuhr prayer is only obligatory at its time), how can it be 'with the command'? The answer resolves by parsing the Arabic: the phrase 'with the command' qualifies 'delivering', not 'obligation'. Thus the delivery (performance) is associated with the command, while the obligation's attachment (to time or command) is not addressed here. For qada', it is delivering something similar to the wajib, also associated with the command. A note adds that some scholars require the condition 'min 'indihi' (from himself, i.e., on his own initiative, not due to another's order) to avoid cases like performing qada of today's prayer for yesterday's, which would be invalid. This subtly touches on the necessity that qada' must be for a missed obligation, not for a future one.

Key Terms
أداء (adā'): Delivering the very same wajib that is commanded, without change, i.e., performing the obligation on time as commanded.
قضاء (qaḍā'): Delivering the like (mithl) of the wajib, with the command, i.e., performing a similar act at a later time as a make-up for a missed obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi commentator resolve the objection that obligation is time-bound?
A) By saying obligation is not time-bound
B) By parsing 'bi al-amr' as attached to 'taslim' not to 'wajib'
C) By denying that time is a condition
D) By redefining ada' as qada'

Key Takeaway
The command (amr) is the source of obligation; time is a condition for performance, not for obligation.

Qada' (Make-up): Definition and the Condition of 'Min `Indihi'
Definition of qada' and a condition for its validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of qada' and explain why the condition 'min `indihi' is necessary.

Core Idea
Qada' is delivering a similar wajib with the command; the condition 'min `indihi' prevents performing qada' for a future obligation.

This lesson examines the definition of qada' as 'taslim mithl al-wajib bi al-amr' and a critical note that some scholars add a condition to avoid invalid applications, such as performing qada' of today's prayer for yesterday.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qada'
Locate 'taslim mithl al-wajib bi al-amr' and note that it requires similarity (mithl) not identity.
2
Understand the condition
Some scholars add 'min `indihi' (from himself) to prevent cases like making up a prayer before its time.
Without this condition, one could claim to perform qada' of a future prayer, which is invalid.
3
Apply the condition
Qada' must be for a missed obligation; the performance must be on own initiative after missing, not anticipatory.
This ensures that qada' is not performed for an obligation that has not yet been missed.
